                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Genome sizes in angiosperms range from 64 Mb in <i>Genlisea</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Greilhuber, J. et al. Smallest angiosperm genomes found in Lentibulariaceae, with chromosomes of bacterial size. Plant Biol. 8, 770–777 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d17717e935">1</a></sup> to an enormous 149 Gb in <i>Paris</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Gregory, T.R. et al. Eukaryotic genome size databases. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, D332–D338 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d17717e941">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Gaut, B.S. &amp; Ross-Ibarra, J. Selection on major components of angiosperm genomes. Science 320, 484–486 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d17717e944">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Pellicer, J., Fay, M.F. &amp; Leitch, I.J. The largest eukaryotic genome of them all? Bot. J. Linn. Soc. 164, 10–15 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d17717e947">4</a></sup>. Two major processes increase genome size: polyploidization and transposable elements proliferation. Processes that counteract genome expansion include the loss of entire chromosomes as well as deletion-biased mutations caused by unequal homologous recombination and illegitimate recombination<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Bennetzen, J.L., Ma, J. &amp; Devos, K.M. Mechanisms of recent genome size variation in flowering plants. Ann. Bot. 95, 127–132 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d17717e951">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Hawkins, J.S., Proulx, S.R., Rapp, R.A. &amp; Wendel, J.F. Rapid DNA loss as a counterbalance to genome expansion through retrotransposon proliferation in plants. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 106, 17811–17816 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d17717e954">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Piegu, B. et al. Doubling genome size without polyploidization: dynamics of retrotransposition-driven genomic expansions in Oryza australiensis, a wild relative of rice. Genome Res. 16, 1262–1269 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d17717e957">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Vitte, C., Panaud, O. &amp; Quesneville, H. LTR retrotransposons in rice (Oryza sativa, L.): recent burst amplifications followed by rapid DNA loss. BMC Genomics 8, 218 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d17717e960">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Woodhouse, M.R. et al. Following tetraploidy in maize, a short deletion mechanism removed genes preferentially from one of the two homologs. PLoS Biol. 8, e1000409 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d17717e963">9</a></sup>. Recent work comparing two cereals, rice and sorghum, has begun to shed light on some of these processes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Paterson, A.H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551–556 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d17717e967">10</a></sup>. However, these species are separated by 60 to 70 million years, making it difficult to disentangle the different evolutionary forces at work.</p><p>An exciting opportunity to understand what drives differences in genome size over shorter time scales is offered by the genus <i>Arabidopsis</i> in the Brassicaceae family. The genome of the self-incompatible perennial <i>A. lyrata</i> is larger than 200 Mb, which is near the family average<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Johnston, J.S. et al. Evolution of genome size in Brassicaceae. Ann. Bot. 95, 229–235 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d17717e980">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Oyama, R.K. et al. The shrunken genome of Arabidopsis thaliana. Plant Syst. Evol. 273, 257–271 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d17717e983">12</a></sup>, whereas the self-compatible annual <i>A. thaliana</i> has one of the smallest angiosperm genomes at about 125 Mb, even though the two species diverged only about 10 million years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Wright, S.I., Lauga, B. &amp; Charlesworth, D. Rates and patterns of molecular evolution in inbred and outbred Arabidopsis. Mol. Biol. Evol. 19, 1407–1420 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d17717e990">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Ossowski, S. et al. The rate and molecular spectrum of spontaneous mutations in Arabidopsis thaliana. Science 327, 92–94 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d17717e993">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Beilstein, M.A., Nagalingum, N.S., Clements, M.D., Manchester, S.R. &amp; Mathews, S. Dated molecular phylogenies indicate a Miocene origin for Arabidopsis thaliana. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 107, 18724–18728 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d17717e996">15</a></sup>. Compared to the difference between the two species, there is much less variation within <i>A. thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Johnston, J.S. et al. Evolution of genome size in Brassicaceae. Ann. Bot. 95, 229–235 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1003">11</a></sup>.</p><p>A high-quality genome sequence for the partially inbred <i>A. lyrata</i> strain MN47 was assembled from approximately 8.3× coverage of dideoxy sequencing reads, making use of information from genetic maps and chromosome painting<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Kuittinen, H. et al. Comparing the linkage maps of the close relatives Arabidopsis lyrata and A. thaliana. Genetics 168, 1575–1584 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1013">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Koch, M.A. &amp; Kiefer, M. Genome evolution among cruciferous plants: a lecture from the comparison of the genetic maps of three diplod species–—Capsella rubella, Arabidopsis lyrata subsp. petraea, and A. thaliana. Am. J. Bot. 92, 761–767 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1016">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Yogeeswaran, K. et al. Comparative genome analyses of Arabidopsis spp.: inferring chromosomal rearrangement events in the evolutionary history of A. thaliana. Genome Res. 15, 505–515 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1019">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Lysak, M.A. et al. Mechanisms of chromosome number reduction in Arabidopsis thaliana and related Brassicaceae species. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 103, 5224–5229 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1022">19</a></sup> (Online Methods). The final assembly included 206.7 Mb of sequence, 90% of which is included in eight large scaffolds covering the majority of each of the eight chromosomes and another large scaffold of 1.9 Mb representing one of the centromeres. Based on cytological observations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Berr, A. et al. Chromosome arrangement and nuclear architecture but not centromeric sequences are conserved between Arabidopsis thaliana and Arabidopsis lyrata. Plant J. 48, 771–783 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1026">20</a></sup>, the centromeric gaps were estimated to span 17.2 Mb. A combination of <i>de novo</i> predictions, homology to <i>A. thaliana</i> features and RNA sequencing was used to annotate the genome. In <i>A. lyrata</i>, we predicted 32,670 protein-coding genes compared to the 27,025 genes in <i>A. thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Swarbreck, D. et al. The Arabidopsis Information Resource (TAIR): gene structure and function annotation. Nucleic Acids Res. 36, D1009–10014 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1042">21</a></sup>.</p><p>Because overall sequence identity between <i>A. lyrata</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i> is greater than 80% (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#MOESM17">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>), the two genomes could be easily aligned (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>). Genetic mapping<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Kuittinen, H. et al. Comparing the linkage maps of the close relatives Arabidopsis lyrata and A. thaliana. Genetics 168, 1575–1584 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1061">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Yogeeswaran, K. et al. Comparative genome analyses of Arabidopsis spp.: inferring chromosomal rearrangement events in the evolutionary history of A. thaliana. Genome Res. 15, 505–515 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1064">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Lysak, M.A. et al. Mechanisms of chromosome number reduction in Arabidopsis thaliana and related Brassicaceae species. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 103, 5224–5229 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1067">19</a></sup> revealed ten major rearrangements, including two reciprocal translocations and three chromosomal fusions, that led to the <i>A. thaliana</i> karyotype of five chromosomes, as compared to the ancestral state of eight chromosomes, as found in <i>A. lyrata</i> and other Brassicaceae. Although centromeric regions are difficult to assemble, we could identify the syntenic region in <i>A. thaliana</i> that corresponds to the chromosome 4 centromere of <i>A. lyrata</i>. The entire centromere has been lost, with only two remnants of satellite repeats in the 1.4-kb intergenic region between <i>AT2G26570</i> and <i>AT2G26580</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#MOESM17">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Comparison of A. lyrata and A. thaliana genomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Comparison of <i>A. lyrata</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i> genomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.807/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.807/MediaObjects/41588_2011_Article_BFng807_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.807/MediaObjects/41588_2011_Article_BFng807_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="282"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Alignment of <i>A. lyrata</i> (<i>Aly</i>) and <i>A. thaliana</i> (<i>Ath</i>) chromosomes. Genomes are scaled to equal size. Only syntenic blocks of at least 500 kb are connected. (<b>b</b>) Orthology classification of genes. (<b>c</b>) Distribution of run lengths of collinear genes. The mode at 1–5 reflects frequent single-gene transpositions. (<b>d</b>) Unalignable sites can be considered as present in one species and absent in the other, as shown in the boxed sequence diagram; matches are indicated by asterisks, and mismatches are indicated by periods. The histogram on the left indicates the absolute number of unalignable sites, and the pie charts in the middle compare their relative distribution over different genomic features. See also <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#MOESM17">Supplementary Table 3</a>. (<b>e</b>) Genome composition (number of elements in parentheses). TEs, transposable elements.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.807/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Apart from chromosomal-scale changes, approximately 90% of the two genomes have remained syntenic, with the great majority being in highly conserved collinear arrangements (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a>). The run-length distribution of collinear gene pairs is bimodal, with a first peak of fragments of five or fewer collinear gene pairs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#Fig1">Fig. 1c</a>), reflecting an abundance of small-scale rearrangements (&lt;10 kb), including single-gene transpositions. Windows containing a breakpoint in collinearity are enriched for transposable elements and other repeats (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#MOESM17">Supplementary Table 1</a>), in agreement with repetitive elements often being associated with chromosomal rearrangements and transposed genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Lim, J.K. &amp; Simmons, M.J. Gross chromosome rearrangements mediated by transposable elements in Drosophila melanogaster. Bioessays 16, 269–275 (1994)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1165">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Stankiewicz, P. et al. Genome architecture catalyzes nonrecurrent chromosomal rearrangements. Am. J. Hum. Genet. 72, 1101–1116 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1168">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Korbel, J.O. et al. Paired-end mapping reveals extensive structural variation in the human genome. Science 318, 420–426 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1171">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Lee, J., Han, K., Meyer, T.J., Kim, H.S. &amp; Batzer, M.A. Chromosomal inversions between human and chimpanzee lineages caused by retrotransposons. PLoS ONE 3, e4047 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1174">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Braumann, I., van den Berg, M.A. &amp; Kempken, F. Strain-specific retrotransposon-mediated recombination in commercially used Aspergillus niger strain. Mol. Genet. Genomics 280, 319–325 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1177">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Woodhouse, M.R., Pedersen, B. &amp; Freeling, M. Transposed genes in Arabidopsis are often associated with flanking repeats. PLoS Genet. 6, e1000949 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1180">27</a></sup>, although they might not necessarily be causal<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Ranz, J.M. et al. Principles of genome evolution in the Drosophila melanogaster species group. PLoS Biol. 5, e152 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1184">28</a></sup>. Two thirds of the 154 inversions identified between the two species are flanked by inverted repeats (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#MOESM17">Supplementary Table 2</a>).</p><p>Despite this overall similarity in gene arrangement, the two genomes are strikingly different in size. A whole-genome alignment revealed that more than 50% (<span class="stix">∼</span>114 Mb) of the <i>A. lyrata</i> genome appears to be missing from the <i>A. thaliana</i> reference genome. In contrast, only about 25% (<span class="stix">∼</span>30 Mb) of the <i>A. thaliana</i> genome is absent from <i>A. lyrata</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#Fig1">Fig. 1d</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#MOESM17">Supplementary Fig. 1e</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#MOESM17">Supplementary Table 3</a>). Nevertheless, the distribution across different sequence classes is similar: half of the unalignable sequences are in transposable elements and a quarter are in intergenic regions. The net effect of these changes is that the <i>A. thaliana</i> genome is <span class="stix">∼</span>80 Mb smaller than the <i>A. lyrata</i> genome, with a much higher fraction of genic sequences (42% instead of 29%), even though the total gene count is smaller (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#Fig1">Fig. 1e</a>). The apparent shrinkage of the <i>A. thaliana</i> genome is not simply because of a few chromosome-scale changes: only 10% of the size difference is attributable to the three missing centromeres, and the rest is due to hundreds of thousands of smaller insertions and deletions spanning all classes of sites. Notably, whereas large differences much more often correspond to sequences only found in <i>A. lyrata</i>, this is not true for very small insertions and deletions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>). This is in stark contrast to genomes from other closely related species with similarly sized genomes, such as chimpanzee and human<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="The Chimpanzee Sequencing and Analysis Consortium. Initial sequence of the chimpanzee genome and comparison with the human genome. Nature 437, 69–87 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1236">29</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Apparent deletions by size and annotation."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Apparent deletions by size and annotation.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.807/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.807/MediaObjects/41588_2011_Article_BFng807_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.807/MediaObjects/41588_2011_Article_BFng807_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="845"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><i>A. lyrata</i> (<i>Aly</i>) is always shown on top, and <i>A. thaliana</i> (<i>Ath</i>) is always shown on the bottom. TE, transposable elements.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.807/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Although rearrangements are correlated with genome shrinkage (rearranged regions are on average shorter in <i>A. thaliana</i> than are collinear regions; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#Fig3">Fig. 3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a>), unalignable sequences are found throughout the genome. An analysis of collinear gene pairs confirmed that in most cases, intergenic intervals in <i>A. lyrata</i> are longer than their counterparts in <i>A. thaliana</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#Fig4">Fig. 4b</a>). Introns behave similarly, although the difference in length is smaller<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Wright, S.I., Lauga, B. &amp; Charlesworth, D. Rates and patterns of molecular evolution in inbred and outbred Arabidopsis. Mol. Biol. Evol. 19, 1407–1420 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1293">13</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Changes in genomic intervals along the A. thaliana genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Changes in genomic intervals along the <i>A. thaliana</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.807/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.807/MediaObjects/41588_2011_Article_BFng807_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.807/MediaObjects/41588_2011_Article_BFng807_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="994"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Mean ratios for all collinear gene pairs in each 100-kb window are shaded in blue, with individual values shown as light blue dots. The ratio of the absolute length of each non-overlapping 100-kb window is shown as a dark purple line. Centromeres are indicated as gray boxes. <i>Aly</i>, <i>A. lyrata</i>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.807/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Change in size of collinear and rearranged regions, intergenic regions and gene families."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Change in size of collinear and rearranged regions, intergenic regions and gene families.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.807/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.807/MediaObjects/41588_2011_Article_BFng807_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.807/MediaObjects/41588_2011_Article_BFng807_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="621"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Size comparison of collinear regions, relative to 100-kb windows in <i>A. thaliana</i>. Asterisks indicate significant differences (binomial test, <i>P</i> &lt; 0.001). (<b>b</b>) Relative size of intergenic intervals. (<b>c</b>) MCL clusters. (<b>d</b>) Relative size of MCL gene families. <i>Ath</i>, <i>A. thaliana</i>; <i>Aly</i>, <i>A. lyrata</i>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.807/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The gene content of <i>A. thaliana</i> is <span class="stix">∼</span>17% lower than that of <i>A. lyrata</i>, but there are no major differences in their Gene Ontology distributions. Similarly, divergence patterns for different gene families between the two species mirror those of within–<i>A. thaliana</i> polymorphism levels<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Clark, R.M. et al. Common sequence polymorphisms shaping genetic diversity in Arabidopsis thaliana. Science 317, 338–342 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1390">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Borevitz, J.O. et al. Genome-wide patterns of single-feature polymorphism in Arabidopsis thaliana. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 104, 12057–12062 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1393">31</a></sup>. The combined gene sets of <i>A. lyrata</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i> result in 12,951 clusters based on the Markov Cluster Algorithm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Enright, A.J., Van Dongen, S. &amp; Ouzounis, C.A. An efficient algorithm for large-scale detection of protein families. Nucleic Acids Res. 30, 1575–1584 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1404">32</a></sup> (MCL), with fewer singletons present in <i>A. thaliana</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#Fig4">Fig. 4c</a>). Among the 8,794 shared multi-gene MCL clusters (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#Fig4">Fig. 4d</a>), clusters that are smaller in <i>A. thaliana</i> outnumber those that are smaller in <i>A. lyrata</i> (1,797 to 612). F-box and NB-LRR genes are examples of gene families with particularly high birth and death rates in plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Clark, R.M. et al. Common sequence polymorphisms shaping genetic diversity in Arabidopsis thaliana. Science 317, 338–342 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1424">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Borevitz, J.O. et al. Genome-wide patterns of single-feature polymorphism in Arabidopsis thaliana. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 104, 12057–12062 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1427">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Michelmore, R.W. &amp; Meyers, B.C. Clusters of resistance genes in plants evolve by divergent selection and a birth-and-death process. Genome Res. 8, 1113–1130 (1998)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1430">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Thomas, J.H. Adaptive evolution in two large families of ubiquitin-ligase adapters in nematodes and plants. Genome Res. 16, 1017–1030 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1433">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Yang, X. et al. The F-box gene family is expanded in herbaceous annual plants relative to woody perennial plants. Plant Physiol. 148, 1189–1200 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1436">35</a></sup>. <i>A. lyrata</i> has 596 F-box and 187 NB-LRR genes, compared to 502 and 159 genes, respectively, in <i>A. thaliana</i>. The trend of fewer genes in <i>A. thaliana</i> is supported by a broader comparison of the <i>Arabidopsis</i> gene set with those of two other dicots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Tuskan, G.A. et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313, 1596–1604 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1453">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1456">37</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Velasco, R. et al. A high quality draft consensus sequence of the genome of a heterozygous grapevine variety. PLoS ONE 2, e1326 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1459">38</a></sup>. <i>A. lyrata</i> has 114 ortholog clusters<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C.J. Jr. &amp; Roos, D.S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178–2189 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1466">39</a></sup> shared with poplar and grapevine but not with <i>A. thaliana</i>, whereas <i>A. thaliana</i> has only 45 clusters found in poplar and grapevine but not in <i>A. lyrata</i>. Similarly, <i>A. lyrata</i> has 875 clusters not detected in any of the other three species, whereas <i>A. thaliana</i> has only 156 species-specific clusters (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#MOESM17">Supplementary Table 4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#MOESM17">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>).</p><p>As in other taxa, transposable elements make an important contribution to the change in genome size (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#Fig1">Fig. 1d</a>), and transposable elements comprise a larger fraction of the <i>A. lyrata</i> genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#Fig1">Fig. 1e</a>). Without an outgroup, one cannot infer directly how much such patterns are shaped by different transposable element activity levels or the differential purging of ancestral transposable elements since speciation. To obtain an estimate of relative activity levels, one can exploit the molecular clock to estimate the average age of long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="SanMiguel, P., Gaut, B.S., Tikhonov, A., Nakajima, Y. &amp; Bennetzen, J.L. The paleontology of intergene retrotransposons of maize. Nat. Genet. 20, 43–45 (1998)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1504">40</a></sup>. Using the experimentally determined mutation rate in <i>A. thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Ossowski, S. et al. The rate and molecular spectrum of spontaneous mutations in Arabidopsis thaliana. Science 327, 92–94 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1510">14</a></sup>, we calculated the mean and median age in <i>A. thaliana</i> to be 3.1 and 2.1 million years, respectively, compared to 1.1 and 0.6 million years, respectively, in <i>A. lyrata</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#Fig5">Fig. 5a</a>). In agreement with previous estimates<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Devos, K.M., Brown, J.K. &amp; Bennetzen, J.L. Genome size reduction through illegitimate recombination counteracts genome expansion in Arabidopsis. Genome Res. 12, 1075–1079 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1524">41</a></sup>, this suggests that LTR retrotransposons have been recently more active in <i>A. lyrata</i>. A phylogenetic analysis also supports a greater expansion of specific LTR retrotransposon clades in <i>A. lyrata</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#Fig5">Fig. 5b</a>). Coupled with higher activity levels of transposable elements in <i>A. lyrata</i>, we found that transposable elements are differently distributed in the two species, with <i>A. lyrata</i> having a higher proportion of genes with a transposable element nearby than <i>A. thaliana</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#Fig5">Fig. 5c</a>), and this distance is skewed toward larger values in <i>A. thaliana</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#MOESM17">Supplementary Table 5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#MOESM17">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>). Together, these observations are consistent with a model under which selection purges transposable elements with deleterious effects on adjacent genes such that transposable elements more distant from genes preferentially survive<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Hollister, J.D. &amp; Gaut, B.S. Epigenetic silencing of transposable elements: a trade-off between reduced transposition and deleterious effects on neighboring gene expression. Genome Res. 19, 1419–1428 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1560">42</a></sup> and with transposable element elimination having been more efficient in <i>A. thaliana</i>. In addition, there is the possibility that transposable elements in <i>A. lyrata</i> have experienced less natural selection because they are on average younger.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Comparison of transposable elements."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5: Comparison of transposable elements.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.807/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.807/MediaObjects/41588_2011_Article_BFng807_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.807/MediaObjects/41588_2011_Article_BFng807_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="812"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Estimated insertion times of LTR retrotransposons based on the experimentally determined mutation rate for <i>A. thaliana</i>. The whiskers indicate values up to 1.5 times the interquartile range. The difference between the species was highly significant (Wilcoxon rank sum test, <i>P</i> &lt; 2.2 × 10<sup>−16</sup>). (<b>b</b>) Phylogeny of Ty1/copia-like and Ty3/gypsy-like LTR retrotransposons. <i>S. cerevisiae</i> Ty1 and Ty3 that were used as outgroups are indicated in green. (<b>c</b>) Distances of nearest transposable elements from each gene. The difference between the two species was not simply because of fewer transposable elements in the <i>A. thaliana</i> genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#MOESM17">Supplementary Table 5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#MOESM17">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>). TE, transposable element; <i>Ath</i>, <i>A. thaliana</i>; <i>Aly</i>, <i>A. lyrata</i>; MYA; million years ago.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.807/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The evidence presented so far points to <i>A. thaliana</i> having suffered a large number of deletions throughout its genome. We can use within-species polymorphisms to shed light on the process by which this has happened. If the <i>A. thaliana</i> genome continues to shrink, we would expect fewer segregating insertions than deletions. Using the <i>A. lyrata</i> genome as a proxy to determine the derived state among a set of insertion and deletion polymorphisms found throughout the genome of 95 <i>A. thaliana</i> plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Nordborg, M. et al. The pattern of polymorphism in Arabidopsis thaliana. PLoS Biol. 3, e196 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1648">43</a></sup>, we found a clear excess of deletions over insertions, with 2,685 fixed and 852 segregating deletions compared to 1,941 fixed and 106 segregating insertions. Furthermore, among the fixed differences, deletions are on average longer than insertions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#Fig6">Fig. 6a</a>). If selection were not involved, and if this pattern was only due to mutational bias favoring deletions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Petrov, D.A., Sangster, T.A., Johnston, J.S., Hartl, D.L. &amp; Shaw, K.L. Evidence for DNA loss as a determinant of genome size. Science 287, 1060–1062 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1656">44</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Petrov, D.A., Lozovskaya, E.R. &amp; Hartl, D.L. High intrinsic rate of DNA loss in Drosophila. Nature 384, 346–349 (1996)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1659">45</a></sup>, deletion and insertion polymorphisms should have similar allele frequencies in the <i>A. thaliana</i> population. However, segregating insertions are on average found in fewer individuals than are deletions or SNPs. Deletions are found in the majority of individuals and many of them are approaching fixation in <i>A. thaliana</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#Fig6">Fig. 6b</a>). This pattern suggests that deletions are favored over insertions because of selection rather than simple mutational bias, thus leading to a smaller genome.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Sizes and allele frequency distribution of insertions and deletions that were either fixed or still segregating in 95 A. thaliana individuals43 and that are presumed to be derived based on comparison with the A. lyrata allele."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 6: Sizes and allele frequency distribution of insertions and deletions that were either fixed or still segregating in 95 <i>A. thaliana</i> individuals<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Nordborg, M. et al. The pattern of polymorphism in Arabidopsis thaliana. PLoS Biol. 3, e196 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1685">43</a></sup> and that are presumed to be derived based on comparison with the <i>A. lyrata</i> allele.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.807/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.807/MediaObjects/41588_2011_Article_BFng807_Fig6_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.807/MediaObjects/41588_2011_Article_BFng807_Fig6_HTML.jpg" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="839"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Size distribution of fixed insertions and deletions. Insertions and deletions that are multiples of a single codon (3 bp) are overrepresented in coding regions. (<b>b</b>) Allele frequency of segregating noncoding insertion and deletion frequencies compared to that of synonymous and non-synonymous polymorphisms.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.807/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The pattern of divergence between the two genomes supports this hypothesis. Although more deletions have occurred on the <i>A. thaliana</i> than the <i>A. lyrata</i> lineage, the bias toward deletions becomes stronger the longer the missing sequence is, and the bias is absent for sequences shorter than 5 bp or so (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>). This is consistent with a model in which long deletions are selectively favored in <i>A. thaliana</i> whereas short deletions are not. We acknowledge that without an outgroup to reconstruct the ancestral state shared by the ancestor of both <i>A. lyrata</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i>, one cannot accurately determine whether all changes are derived in <i>A. thaliana</i>.</p><p>In summary, we have presented a high-quality reference genome sequence for <i>A. lyrata</i>, which will be a valuable resource for functional, evolutionary and ecological studies in the genus <i>Arabidopsis</i>. Several processes contribute to the remarkable difference in genome size between the predominantly selfing <i>A. thaliana</i> and the outcrossing <i>A. lyrata</i>. In just a few million generations, numerous chromosomal rearrangements have occurred, consistent with theoretical predictions of rearrangements that reduce fitness in heterozygotes being fixed much more easily in strongly selfing species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Charlesworth, B. Evolutionary rates in partially self-fertilizing species. Am. Nat. 140, 126–148 (1992)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1750">46</a></sup>. Though <i>A. thaliana</i> has 17% fewer genes than <i>A. lyrata</i>, much of the genome size difference seems to be caused by reduced transposable element activity and/or more efficient transposable element elimination in <i>A. thaliana</i>, especially near genes, as well as shortening of non–transposable element intergenic sequences and introns in <i>A. thaliana</i>. Specifically, by making the reasonable assumption that the <i>A. lyrata</i> allele presents in the majority of cases the ancestral state, we found that segregating deletions at non-coding sites in <i>A. thaliana</i> are skewed toward higher allele frequencies and that both fixed and polymorphic deletions are more common than insertions. Together, this suggests pervasive selection for a smaller genome in <i>A. thaliana</i>. Apart from apparent advantages for species with smaller genomes that have been inferred from meta-analyses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Knight, C.A., Molinari, N.A. &amp; Petrov, D.A. The large genome constraint hypothesis: evolution, ecology and phenotype. Ann. Bot. 95, 177–190 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1777">47</a></sup>, the transition to selfing might be an important factor in this process<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Charlesworth, B. Evolutionary rates in partially self-fertilizing species. Am. Nat. 140, 126–148 (1992)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1781">46</a></sup>. In addition, a shorter life span may allow a reduction of the genetic repertoire and thus contribute to the smaller genome of <i>A. thaliana</i> as well.</p><p>What role, if any, genome expansion might play in <i>A. lyrata</i> can be addressed once detailed <i>A. lyrata</i> polymorphism information as well as closely related outgroup genomes become available, such as the one from <i>Capsella rubella</i>, which is currently being assembled. A complete understanding of the processes behind genome contraction and expansion over short time scales will also require better knowledge of mutational events and a deeper understanding of the distribution of, and selection on, noncoding regulatory sequences<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Hollister, J.D. &amp; Gaut, B.S. Epigenetic silencing of transposable elements: a trade-off between reduced transposition and deleterious effects on neighboring gene expression. Genome Res. 19, 1419–1428 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1800">42</a></sup>. For both, high-quality whole-genome sequences of additional <i>Arabidopsis</i> relatives will be an important tool.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec2">URLs.</h3><p>MCL, <a href="http://www.micans.org/mcl/">http://www.micans.org/mcl/</a>; At_RGenes, <a href="http://niblrrs.ucdavis.edu/At_RGenes/">http://niblrrs.ucdavis.edu/At_RGenes/</a>; RepeatModeler, <a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler.html">http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler.html</a>; EMBOSS, <a href="http://emboss.sourceforge.net/">http://emboss.sourceforge.net/</a>; RepBase, <a href="http://www.girinst.org/server/RepBase/">http://www.girinst.org/server/RepBase/</a>; JCVI/TIGR plant repeat database, <a href="http://blast.jcvi.org/euk-blast/index.cgi?project=plant.repeats">http://blast.jcvi.org/euk-blast/index.cgi?project=plant.repeats</a>; PHYTOZOME portal, <a href="http://www.phytozome.net/alyrata.php">http://www.phytozome.net/alyrata.php</a>; GenomeThreader, <a href="http://www.genomethreader.org/">http://www.genomethreader.org/</a>.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec4">Accession codes.</h4><p>The assembly and annotation (Entrez Genome Project ID 41137) are available from GenBank (accession number <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sites/entrez?db=genome&amp;cmd=search&amp;term=ADBK00000000">ADBK00000000</a>) and from JGI's PHYTOZOME portal (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#Sec2">URLs</a>). Seeds from the MN47 strain have been deposited with the <i>Arabidopsis</i> Biological Resource Center under accession number <a href="http://www.arabidopsis.org/servlet/TairObject?id=2501543193&amp;type=stock">CS22696</a>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec5" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec5-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec5">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec5-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Sequencing and assembly.</h3><p><i>Arabidopsis lyrata</i> strain MN47 was derived by forced selfing from material collected in Michigan, USA by C. Langley (University of California Davis). It was inbred five times before DNA was extracted for sequencing. Libraries with various insert sizes including fosmids and bacterial artificial chromosomes (BACs) were dideoxy sequenced on ABI 3730XL capillary sequencers. Reads were assembled with Arachne<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Jaffe, D.B. et al. Whole-genome sequence assembly for mammalian genomes: Arachne 2. Genome Res. 13, 91–96 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1914">48</a></sup>, and collinearity information was integrated with marker information from genetic maps<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Kuittinen, H. et al. Comparing the linkage maps of the close relatives Arabidopsis lyrata and A. thaliana. Genetics 168, 1575–1584 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1918">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Yogeeswaran, K. et al. Comparative genome analyses of Arabidopsis spp.: inferring chromosomal rearrangement events in the evolutionary history of A. thaliana. Genome Res. 15, 505–515 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1921">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Lysak, M.A. et al. Mechanisms of chromosome number reduction in Arabidopsis thaliana and related Brassicaceae species. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 103, 5224–5229 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1924">19</a></sup> to reconstruct the eight linkage groups. Additional details and specifics are presented in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#MOESM17">Supplementary Note</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Annotation.</h3><p>The genome was annotated using <i>ab initio</i> and homology-based gene predictors along with RNA-Seq data (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#MOESM17">Supplementary Note</a>). The complete details are described in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#MOESM17">Supplementary Note</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">MCL cluster analyses.</h3><p>MCL (mcl-06-058 package; see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#Sec2">URLs</a>) was used with default parameters (-I 2, -S 6) based on clustering of hits with <i>E</i> value ≤ 10<sup>−5</sup>. MCL uses a Markov cluster algorithm that attempts to overcome many of the difficulties with protein sequence clustering, such as the presence of multi-domain proteins, peptide fragments and proteins with very common domains. This method has been used for a variety of animal genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Demuth, J.P., De Bie, T., Stajich, J.E., Cristianini, N. &amp; Hahn, M.W. The evolution of mammalian gene families. PLoS ONE 1, e85 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1964">49</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Prachumwat, A. &amp; Li, W.H. Gene number expansion and contraction in vertebrate genomes with respect to invertebrate genomes. Genome Res. 18, 221–232 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1967">50</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Drosophila 12 Genomes Consortium. et al. Evolution of genes and genomes on the Drosophila phylogeny. Nature 450, 203–218 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1970">51</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">OrthoMCL analysis.</h3><p>Orthologous gene clusters were computed from OrthoMCL comparisons<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C.J. Jr. &amp; Roos, D.S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178–2189 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1982">39</a></sup> of four dicotyledonous species with finished genomes: <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>A. lyrata</i>, <i>Populus trichocarpa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Tuskan, G.A. et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313, 1596–1604 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d17717e1994">36</a></sup> and <i>Vitis vinifera</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d17717e2001">37</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Velasco, R. et al. A high quality draft consensus sequence of the genome of a heterozygous grapevine variety. PLoS ONE 2, e1326 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d17717e2004">38</a></sup>. A search for potentially missed genes in both <i>Arabidopsis</i> genomes resulted in minor adjustments of the OrthoMCL clusters. Instead of 10,573 clusters, 10,878 clusters now contained at least one gene from each the four species, and instead of 5,699 clusters, 5,800 clusters were <i>Arabidopsis</i> specific. To determine deleted or newly generated orthologs (by OrthoMCL definition) between the two species, we focused on clusters specific for either <i>A. lyrata</i> or <i>A. thaliana</i>. For both species, there are two cluster types: those that are supported by members in <i>P. trichocarpa</i> and/or <i>V. vinifera</i> (supported specific cluster (SSC)) and clusters exclusively found in one of the <i>Arabidopsis</i> species (exclusive specific cluster (ESC)). We did not consider 2,939 and 6,103 unclustered genes (singletons) in <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>A. lyrata</i>, respectively.</p><p>In our initial analysis, we detected 354 SSCs and 161 ESCs for <i>A. thaliana</i> and 168 SSCs and 833 ESCs for <i>A. lyrata</i>. Whole-genome projects, however, may contain false-positive as well as missed or incomplete partial gene calls that impose difficulties for OrthoMCL to detect orthologous relationships. To ensure that genes from the previously detected SSCs were indeed specific for one of the <i>Arabidopsis</i> species, we reevaluated the absence or presence of specific gene calls in the two genome sequences. Previously missed genes detected by GenomeThreader (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#Sec2">URLs</a>) were added to each of the gene sets and the OrthoMCL analysis was repeated.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">F-box and NB-LRR gene analysis.</h3><p>Using F-box PF00646.hmm as an HMM profile with hmmsearch (<i>E</i> value ≤ 10<sup>−5</sup>), 394 hits were found in <i>A. thaliana</i> and 461 hits were found in <i>A. lyrata</i>. Alignment of these sequences was optimized with the PF00646 seed using ClustalX 2.0 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Thompson, J.D., Gibson, T.J., Plewniak, F., Jeanmougin, F. &amp; Higgins, D.G. The CLUSTAL-X Windows interface: flexible strategies for multiple sequence alignment aided by quality analysis tools. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 4876–4882 (1997)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d17717e2070">52</a>). The final alignment was produced by aligning with hmmalign against PF00646.hmm to construct an <i>Arabidopsis</i>-specific HMM F-box profile. With this HMM profile, 502 hits were found in <i>A. thaliana</i>, and 596 hits were found in <i>A. lyrata</i>. hmmalign was used to align all of these against PF00646.hmm.</p><p>A blastp search (<i>E</i> value ≤ 10<sup>−10</sup>) was performed with the nucleotide-binding site (NB) domain (based on HMMEMIT, from At_RGenes; see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#Sec2">URLs</a>). The NB domains of the retrieved proteins (142 in <i>A. thaliana</i> and 162 in <i>A. lyrata</i>) were aligned using ClustalX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Thompson, J.D., Gibson, T.J., Plewniak, F., Jeanmougin, F. &amp; Higgins, D.G. The CLUSTAL-X Windows interface: flexible strategies for multiple sequence alignment aided by quality analysis tools. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 4876–4882 (1997)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d17717e2102">52</a></sup>. This alignment was used to develop an <i>Arabidopsis</i>-specific HMM profile, which was used to search the complete set of proteins encoded by both the two genomes (cutoff <i>E</i> ≤ 10<sup>−5</sup>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">RepeatMasker analyses.</h3><p>To develop <i>de novo</i> repeat libraries for both species, we used RepeatModeler (version Beta 1.0.3; see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#Sec2">URLs</a>). To reduce false positives, unclassified repeats were compared to annotated genes, and we eliminated all that had at least 80% identity to annotated genes over at least 80 bp (GenBank: green plant GB all (protein); blastx with <i>E</i> value ≤ 10<sup>−10</sup>). The remaining RepeatModeler predictions were classified with the 80-80-80 rule<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Wicker, T. et al. A unified classification system for eukaryotic transposable elements. Nat. Rev. Genet. 8, 973–982 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d17717e2133">53</a></sup>, grouping repeats if they shared at least 80% identity over at least 80% of the aligned sequence, which had to be at least 80 bp long. The identified repeats were appended to RepBase (<i>Arabidopsis</i> library, RM database version 20080611) resulting in a final library with 1,152 repeat units. The final libraries were used to annotate transposable elements using RepeatMasker version 3.2.5.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">LTR retrotransposons.</h3><p>Intact LTR retrotransposons were identified <i>de novo</i> using LTR_STRUC<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="McCarthy, E.M. &amp; McDonald, J.F. LTR_STRUC: a novel search and identification program for LTR retrotransposons. Bioinformatics 19, 362–367 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d17717e2152">54</a></sup> with default parameters. Based on the sequence divergence between the two LTRs of the same element, insertion times were estimated. All LTR pairs were aligned using MUSCLE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Edgar, R.C. MUSCLE: a multiple sequence alignment method with reduced time and space complexity. BMC Bioinformatics 5, 113 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d17717e2156">55</a></sup>, and the distance <i>K</i> between them was calculated with the Kimura two-parameter model using the distmat program implemented in the EMBOSS package (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#Sec2">URLs</a>). The insertion time <i>T</i> was calculated as <i>T = K</i>/(2) (<i>r</i>), with <i>r</i> as the rate of nucleotide substitution. The molecular clock was set based on the observed mutation rate of 7 × 10<sup>−9</sup> per site per generation (assumed to equal one year)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Ossowski, S. et al. The rate and molecular spectrum of spontaneous mutations in Arabidopsis thaliana. Science 327, 92–94 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d17717e2181">14</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Classification and phylogeny of LTR retrotransposons.</h3><p>LTR retrotransposons can be classified into Ty1/copia-like and Ty3/gypsy-like elements<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Xiong, Y. &amp; Eickbush, T.H. Origin and evolution of retroelements based upon their reverse transcriptase sequences. EMBO J. 9, 3353–3362 (1990)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d17717e2193">56</a></sup>. We classified repeats using RepBase (version 13.08; see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#Sec2">URLs</a>) and blastn (<i>E</i> value ≤ 10<sup>−10</sup>) and by direct comparison against the JCVI/TIGR plant repeat database (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#Sec2">URLs</a>). All intact LTR retrotransposons were compared with blastx (<i>E</i> value ≤ 10<sup>−10</sup>) against a conserved 156-amino-acid segment corresponding to the reverse transcriptase domain<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Zhang, X. &amp; Wessler, S.R. Genome-wide comparative analysis of the transposable elements in the related species Arabidopsis thaliana and Brassica oleracea. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 101, 5589–5594 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d17717e2214">57</a></sup> of Ty1/copia-like and Ty3/gypsy-like sequences, and this segment was then used for phylogenetic reconstruction using PAUP* version 4.0b10 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Swofford, D.L. PAUP*: Phylogenetic Analysis Using Parsimony (*and Other Methods): Version 4. (Sinauer Associates, Sunderland, Massachusetts, USA, 2003)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d17717e2217">58</a>) and a neighbor-joining method. As the outgroup sequence, we used the reverse transcriptase domain from yeast Ty1 and Ty3 elements, respectively<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Zhang, X. &amp; Wessler, S.R. Genome-wide comparative analysis of the transposable elements in the related species Arabidopsis thaliana and Brassica oleracea. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 101, 5589–5594 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d17717e2221">57</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Detection and analysis of chromosomal breakpoints.</h3><p>Genome-wide collinearity was detected by running i-ADHoRe<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Simillion, C., Vandepoele, K., Saeys, Y. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. Building genomic profiles for uncovering segmental homology in the twilight zone. Genome Res. 14, 1095–1106 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d17717e2233">59</a></sup> on the core-orthologous genes, allowing for the identification of breakpoints, including inversions and nested inversions. For each inversion, the regions 10 kb upstream and downstream of the delimiting breakpoints were compared to each other using blastn (word size 4), tblastx (word size 1) and SSEARCH<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Altschul, S.F. et al. Gapped BLAST and PSI-BLAST: a new generation of protein database search programs. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 3389–3402 (1997)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d17717e2237">60</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Smith, T.F. &amp; Waterman, M.S. Identification of common molecular subsequences. J. Mol. Biol. 147, 195–197 (1981)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d17717e2240">61</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Pearson, W.R. Searching protein sequence libraries: comparison of the sensitivity and selectivity of the Smith-Waterman and FASTA algorithms. Genomics 11, 635–650 (1991)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d17717e2243">62</a></sup>. Tblastx outperforms blastn for coding regions. In noncoding regions, SSEARCH is more sensitive than blastn but is computationally less efficient and, hence, is the most useful for comparison of shorter sequences. Only one hit per strand was reported. Therefore, for each pair of inversion flanking regions, all combinations of repeats and protein coding genes were evaluated. Default settings were used for gap penalties. An <i>E</i> value of ≤ 0.01 was considered as indicating similarity between the upstream and downstream regions.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Similarity of syntenic regions.</h3><p>To investigate the nucleotide divergence of intergenic regions around coding genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#MOESM17">Supplementary Fig. 1b</a>), we extracted for each syntenic gene pair the 2-kb sequences 5′ of the start codon and 3′ from the stop codon. If the neighboring gene was closer than 2 kb, the extracted sequence was accordingly trimmed. Coding sequences of syntenic genes were also analyzed. Global alignments of syntenic sequences were generated using the Needleman-Wunsch algorithm as implemented in the EMBOSS package 5.0 (using default parameters). The sequence identity of the coding regions was measured over the full-length alignment. To investigate whether the divergence of an intergenic sequence is affected by the relative orientation to neighboring genes, upstream sequences were split into head-to-tail and head-to-head groups, and downstream sequences were split into tail-to-head and tail-to-tail groups.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Fixed insertions and deletions.</h3><p>To identify fixed insertions and deletions among 1,238 fragments that had been amplified by PCR and sequenced in 95 <i>A. thaliana</i> individuals<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Nordborg, M. et al. The pattern of polymorphism in Arabidopsis thaliana. PLoS Biol. 3, e196 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d17717e2273">43</a></sup>, two representative sequences for each fragment were first constructed to represent the insertion and deletion states among all segregating indels. The representative sequence consisting of insertions was then queried against the <i>A. lyrata</i> genome with both BLAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Kent, W.J. BLAT–—the BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656–664 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d17717e2280">63</a></sup> (-maxGap = 100 -extendThroughN -minIdentity = 80) and BLAST<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Altschul, S.F., Gish, W., Miller, W., Myers, E.W. &amp; Lipman, D.J. Basic local alignment search tool. J. Mol. Biol. 215, 403–410 (1990)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d17717e2284">64</a></sup> (-e 0.00001 -F F -G -5 -E -1). Based on the longest hit from the union of hits obtained by both methods, the representative sequences for each alignment were profile aligned with the <i>A. lyrata</i> allele with MAFFT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Katoh, K., Asimenos, G. &amp; Toh, H. Multiple alignment of DNA sequences with MAFFT. Methods Mol. Biol. 537, 39–64 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.807#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d17717e2292">65</a></sup>. Fixed insertions and deletions were identified in the resulting alignment.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Segregating insertions and deletions.</h3><p>A similar procedure to that described above was used to identify the <i>A. lyrata</i> allele (presumed ancestral state) for each polymorphic indel in <i>A. thaliana</i>. Instead of querying the entire fragment, we queried each insertion allele along with 25 bp flanking each side against the <i>A. lyrata</i> genome using BLAT. For each polymorphic indel, we filtered for the best hit that spanned both sides of the indel site (by at least 3 bp) and reported each indel as either a derived insertion (if the <i>A. lyrata</i> allele was a deletion in the resulting profile alignment) or a derived deletion (if the <i>A. lyrata</i> allele was not a deletion).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Data and seed availability.</h3><p>The assembly and annotation (Entrez Genome Project ID 41137) are available from GenBank (see Accession Codes section) and from JGI's PHYTOZOME portal (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.807#Sec2">URLs</a>). Seeds of the MN47 strain have been deposited with the <i>Arabidopsis</i> Biological Resource Center (see Accession Codes section).</p></div></div></section>

Bennetzen, J. Ma, KM. Devos, " /><meta itemprop="datePublished" content="2005" /><meta itemprop="headline" content="Bennetzen, J.L., Ma, J. &amp; Devos, K.M. Mechanisms of recent genome size variation in flowering plants. Ann.
